    宋人战马倒被擒不知存殁也【案金史本宋兵大至金兵败阿林战殁赠金紫光禄大夫西京留守与此志不知存殁有异】南渡后诸女直世袭明安穆昆往往好文学与士大夫游如完顔色埒兄弟伊喇廷玉温甫总领尔佳徳固【案尔佳旧作夹谷今改】珠格士乌凌阿肃孺辈作诗多有可称徳固勇悍在军中有声尝送舍弟以诗亦可喜天兴初提兵戍谯军乱见杀
    　　南渡之后为将帅者多出于世家皆膏粱乳臭子若完顔巴萨【案巴萨旧作白撒今改】止以能打毬称又完顔额尔克【案额尔克旧作讹可今改】亦以能打毬号板子元帅者又完顔鼎努【案鼎努旧作定奴今改】号三脆羮有以忮忍号火燎元帅者又赫舍哩雅尔呼达【案旧作纥石烈牙忽亦作纥石烈牙吾塔今改】号卢鼓椎好用鼓椎击人也其人本出亲军颇勇悍镇宿泗数年屡破宋兵有威好结小人心然跋扈不受朝廷制尝入朝诣都堂诋毁宰执宰执亦不敢言而人主倚其镇东亦优容之也尤不喜文士僚属有长裾者辄

    Explanation

    101

    A Song Dynasty warrior's horse was captured, and it is unknown if he is alive or dead. [According to the History of the Jin Dynasty, when the Song army arrived in large numbers, the Jin army was defeated, and Arlin was killed in battle, posthumously awarded the title of Gold Purple Grand Master and Western Capital Governor, which differs from the record here of unknown survival.]

    After the Southern Migration, many Jurchen (女真) nobles who inherited titles such as Ming'an and Mukun often loved literature and associated with literati, such as the brothers Wanyan Celi and Irula Tingyu Wenfu, the leader Querjia DeGu [Note: Querjia was formerly written as JiaGu and has been changed], Zhuge Shi, and the likes of Wulinga Suru, who composed poems that were often praised. DeGu was brave and fierce, renowned in the military, and once sent a poem to his younger brother, which was also delightful. In the early Tianxing era, he led troops to garrison at Qiao, where he was killed in a military rebellion.

    After the Southern Migration, many generals came from noble families, all pampered and inexperienced young men. For example, Wanyan Basa [Note: Basa was formerly written as BaSa and has been changed], only known for being good at polo, and Wanyan Eerke [Note: Eerke was formerly written as Eke and has been changed], also known for being good at polo, were both called "Board Commander." Wanyan Dingnu [Note: Dingnu was formerly written as Dinnu and has been changed] was known as "Three Fragile Soup," and another was called "Flame Commander" due to his jealous and cruel nature. Another was called "Lu Drum Hammer" because he liked to use a drum hammer to strike people. This person originally came from the imperial guard, was very brave and fierce, and garrisoned in Su and Si for several years, repeatedly defeating the Song army and gaining renown. He was good at winning the hearts of the common people but was rebellious and not subject to imperial control. He once entered the capital to attend court and criticized the chief ministers, who dared not speak up, while the emperor relied on his ability to govern the east and tolerated him. He particularly disliked scholars and officials who wore long robes, always treating them harshly.

    取刀截去又喜凌侮使者凡朝廷遣使者来必以酒食困之或辞以不饮因并食不给使饿而去张用章尝以司农少卿行户部过宿见焉雅尔呼达召饮张辞以有寒疾雅尔呼达笑曰此易治耳趣命左右持艾炷来当筵令人拉张卧遽艾于腹张不能争遂灸数十又因防宴诸将并妻皆在座时共食猪肉馒首有一将妻言素不食猪肉雅尔呼达趣左右易之须防食讫问曰尔食何肉其人对曰相公易以羊肉甚美雅尔呼达笑曰不食猪肉而食人肉何也尔所食非羊肉也其人大呕疾病数日又御史大夫和卓因事过宿【案和卓旧作合住今

    102

    A man who enjoyed cutting off parts of people and bullying envoys. Whenever the imperial court sent envoys, they were always exhausted with alcohol and food, or they would refuse to drink and be left without food, forced to leave hungry. Zhang Yongzhang, who served as a deputy minister of agriculture and temporarily handled the Ministry of Revenue, once stayed overnight and visited him. Yarhuda invited him to drink, but Zhang declined, citing a cold illness. Yarhuda laughed and said, "This is easy to treat. Quickly order the attendants to bring moxa cones." At the banquet, he had someone pull Zhang down and quickly applied moxa to his abdomen. Zhang could not resist and was subjected to dozens of moxibustions. Another time, during a banquet for generals and their wives, they all ate pork buns. One of the generals' wives said she never ate pork. Yarhuda quickly ordered the attendants to replace it. After the banquet, he asked, "What kind of meat did you eat?" She replied, "The nobleman replaced it with mutton, which was very delicious." Yarhuda laughed and said, "If you don't eat pork, why do you eat human meat? The meat you ate was not mutton." She then vomited severely and fell ill for several days. Another time, the Grand Censor He Zhuo stayed overnight due to some matter.

    改】雅尔呼达馆之酒肉使妓歌于前及夜因使其妓侍寝迟明将发令妓征钱和卓愕然雅尔呼达因彊发其箧笥取缯帛悉以付妓曰岂有官使人而不与钱者乎和卓无以对而去故司农御史皆不敢入其境避之又宿州有营妓数人皆其所喜者时时使一妓佩银符屡往州郡取赇赂州将夫人皆逺迎号省差行首厚赠之其横若此及康锡伯禄为御史上章言其事且曰朝廷容之适所以害之欲保全其人宜加裁制然朝廷竟不能治其罪后北兵入境移镇京兆军败召还道病死在东方时卢鼓椎之名满民间儿啼亦可怖大槩如呼玛呼云

    Explanation

    103

    In the residence of Yaerhuda, he provided food and entertainment for his guests, including singing and dancing, and at night, he arranged for his courtesans to accompany them to sleep. Early the next morning, as they were about to depart, one of the courtesans demanded payment from Hozhuo, who was surprised and unable to respond. Yaerhuda then forced Hozhuo to open his chest and handed over all the silk and cloth to the courtesan, saying, "How can an official send a messenger without giving him money?" Hozhuo had no reply and left. As a result, officials such as the Commissioner of Agriculture and the Censor General were afraid to enter his territory and avoided him. In Suzhou, there were several courtesans whom Yaerhuda favored, and he would often send one of them with a silver token to collect bribes from various places. The local officials and their wives would greet her warmly and give her generous gifts. The extent of Yaerhuda's corruption was such that when Kang Xibo, a censor, reported the matter to the court, he said, "The court's tolerance of him is actually harming him. To preserve him, he should be subject to stricter control." However, the court could not punish him. Later, when northern troops entered the region, he was transferred to the capital, but his army was defeated, and he was recalled. On his way back, he fell ill and died. In the east, the name of Lu Guchui was so notorious that even children's cries were frightening, and it was generally as terrifying as calling out "Huma Hu."

    104

    任履真字子山许州长人读书喜杂学深于医又有善行邑人皆信之贞祐初召入太医院旋告归与闲闲屏山诸公及余先子善先子主长葛簿修儒宫及太虚观子山之力居多为医起人疾甚众既卒闲闲志其墓云
    　　张子和睢州考城人初名从正精于医贯穿难素之学厯厯在口其法宗刘守真完素药多用寒凉然起疾救死多取效士大夫称焉为人放诞无威仪颇读书作诗嗜酒久居陈游余先子门后召入太医院旋告去然名重东州麻知防九畴与之善使子和论説其术因为文之有六门三法之目将行于世防子和知防相继死迄今其书存焉
    　　僧徳普武川人自号胜静老人倜傥有机术与士大夫游饮酒食肉豁如也尝为珠赫髙琪所重在军中论兵南渡居陈之开元寺与余先子善尝着弥陀偈谈理性先子为序之屏山亦喜其俊爽不羁也颇喜字画作诗年六十余死余谓古之文畅秘演之流

    104

    Ren Lishen, styled Zishan, was from Changli, Xu Prefecture. He enjoyed a wide range of studies and was deeply skilled in medicine, with many good deeds that earned the trust of the people in his hometown. In the early years of Zhengyou, he was summoned to the Imperial Medical Bureau but soon requested to return home. He befriended the gentlemen of Xianxian, Pingshan, and my late father. My late father served as the magistrate of Changge, overseeing the construction of Confucian temples and the Taixu Temple, with Ren Lishen contributing significantly to these efforts. He cured many people of their illnesses and was highly regarded by Xianxian, who wrote his epitaph.

    Zhang Zhe was from Kaocun, Suizhou. Originally named Congzheng, he was skilled in medicine and thoroughly versed in the studies of the Nanjing and Suwen. His methods were clearly articulated and followed those of Liu Shuzhen and Wan Su, often using cold and cooling herbs. He frequently succeeded in curing diseases and saving lives, earning praise from the literati. Zhang Zhe was unrestrained and lacked formal dignity, but he read extensively and composed poetry. He loved drinking and lived in Chen, befriending my late father. He was summoned to the Imperial Medical Bureau but soon requested to leave, yet he was highly regarded in the eastern region. Ma Zhifang Jiu Chou befriended him and had Zhang Zhe explain his methods, compiling a book titled "Six Categories and Three Methods," which he intended to publish. However, Zhang Zhe and Ma Zhifang died one after another, and to this day, his book still exists.

    Monk De Pu was from Wuchuan and self-styled as Shengjing Elder. He was bold and resourceful, associating with scholars and officials. He drank and ate meat without restraint and was highly regarded by Zhu He Gao Qi. He discussed military strategy during the Southern Migration and lived in the Kaiyuan Temple in Chen, befriending my late father. He composed a Mita verse discussing rationality, for which my late father wrote a preface. Pingshan also admired his bold and unrestrained nature. He enjoyed calligraphy and poetry and died in his sixties. I consider him in the tradition of ancient writers like Wen Chang and Miyi.

    僧圆基字子初姓田氏亦北人虽为浮屠善与豪士游负其材略有握兵治民之意葢于僧者也尝住持南京静安寺以不检去之岘山厯嵩阳死与徳普相善颇能诗尝题伊喇右丞画云调燮之余总是闲闲中游戏到毫端而今亦有丹青手犹在磻溪把钓竿可见其有志也又咏栁叶云一气潜通造化中人间无处不春风莫嫌冷地开青眼试看夭桃防日红
    　　王赤腿不知其名字年齿人以其衣短号哨腿王或云名予可字南云河东人幼尝为卒不谨居郾蔡间以乞食为事衣皮衣露膝长叹好挿花额上系一铜片如月人问之皆有説又时时自言为天帝所召有某仙某神在焉所食何物皆诞诡莫可测然善歌诗有求之者索韵立成字亦怪异在郾城凡寺观楼阁及民家屋壁书其诗殆遍往往有竒丽语如天仙有梦梅云鼎铸陶钧政格新横斜疎影慰骚魂婴香枕簟黄昬月懋棣东风笑谷春又经闲璈几虚云锁杯卷江山枕岛楼却忆西岩旧宫殿半横星斗下瀛洲又题石潭云石裂雯华浸月秋又松隂

    Explanation

    105

    Monk Yuanji, also known as Zichu, was a native of the north whose family name was Tian. Although he was a monk, he was adept at associating with influential people and had a talent with a slight inclination towards governing people and managing troops. He was somewhat unconventional for a monk. He once served as the abbot of Jing'an Temple in Nanjing but was dismissed due to his lack of discipline. He later lived in Xian Mountain and then in Songyang. He died and was well-known for his friendship with Dēpǔ. He was skilled in poetry and once inscribed on a painting by Yi La, the Right Chancellor, the lines: "In the leisure of governance, all is play; in the brush, games abound. Even today, there are those who wield the brush, still fishing at Panshi Creek." This shows his aspirations. He also wrote a poem about willow leaves: "All things are subtly connected in the creation of heaven and earth; there is no place in the world where the spring breeze does not reach. Do not disdain the cold; open your eyes wide. Look at the peach blossoms blooming in the morning sun."

    Wang Chitu, whose name, age, and origin are unknown, was called Wang the Short-Legged because of his short clothing. Some say his name was Yu Ke and his courtesy name was Nan Yun. He was from Hedong and had once served as a soldier but was not careful in his conduct, living in the regions of Yan and Cai where he begged for food. He wore a fur coat, exposing his knees, and often sighed, enjoying wearing flowers in his hair and attaching a copper plate shaped like the moon to his forehead. When people asked him about it, he had explanations for everything. He often claimed to be summoned by the Heavenly Emperor and said that certain immortals and gods were present with him. What he ate was always fantastical and hard to measure, yet he was skilled in singing and poetry. Those who sought him would ask for a rhyme, and he would compose it instantly. His writing was also strange. In Yan Cheng, he inscribed his poems on the walls of temples, pavilions, towers, and even the houses of common people, and they were everywhere. Often, his poems contained beautiful and unusual phrases, such as: "Dreaming of plum blossoms, there are heavenly maidens. The cauldron casts and the pottery is refined, the politics are new. The sparse shadows of slanting branches comfort the soul of the poet. Fragrant pillows and mats in the twilight moonlight, the east wind laughs in the spring of the valley. The idle zither on the table is empty, the cup is locked, the mountains and rivers are in the island tower, recalling the old palace of the Western Rock, half lying under the stars and the moon in the sea." He also inscribed on a stone pond: "The stone cracks, the wén hua (a type of flower) is immersed in the autumn moon." And on the shade of a pine tree.

    滚碎阑干角其他多僻怪不可晓问之则曰出天上何书书名亦不可晓或云为物所凭麻知防独重之李子迁赠诗云肮脏风仪古丈夫袍铁面防髭人间春色向头剰天上月明当额孤石鼎夜聨诗句健布囊春醉酒钱粗危楼试倚街头月应见潜飞入玉壶状其人殆尽正大初余过郾诸公为召至索诗求韵立书辞亦不可晓后因病卒【后因病失一目明遭乱北渡病死　案大金国志遭乱为顺天军将领所得知其名议欲挈之北归馆于州之瑞云观明日见将领自言曰我不欲住君家瑞云观也越三日卒后有见之淮上者与此志可以攷异】

    Explanation

    106

    A Man of Strange and Obscure Ways

    There was a man whose manner was that of an ancient, dignified man, with a face like iron and a beard. His appearance was such that the spring scenery of the world was still present on his head, and the bright moon of heaven shone alone on his forehead. At night, he composed vigorous verses by the stone tripod, and in spring, he got drunk and carried his money in a coarse cloth bag. He would lean on the moon at the top of a tall building near the street, and it was said that he should see the moon fly silently into a jade vase. This man was Ma Zhifang, who alone valued him. Li Ziqian wrote a poem in his honor, describing him as "a man of ancient, dignified bearing, with a face like iron and a beard, whose spring scenery remains on his head, and whose bright moon shines alone on his forehead."

    During the early years of Zhengda, I passed through Yan, and the scholars there invited me to compose poetry. They asked for a rhyme, and I wrote it down, but it is also not understood. Later, he died of illness. According to the Ta Jin Guo Zhi (History of the Great Jin Dynasty), he was captured by a general of the Shuntian Army during chaos, and the general wanted to take him north to the State of Ruyun Temple. The next day, the man told the general, "I do not want to stay in your home, Ruyun Temple." Three days later, he died. Later, someone saw him in Huai, and this account can be compared with the Ta Jin Guo Zhi to find discrepancies.

    归潜志巻六
    <子部,小说家类,杂事之属,归潜志>
    　　钦定四库全书
    　　归潜志巻七
    　　元　刘祁　撰
    　　兴定初珠格髙琪为相建议南京城分八十里极大难守子内再筑子城周方四十里壊民屋舍甚众工役大兴河南之民皆以为苦又使朝官监役分督方面少不前辄杖之【案金史宣宗本纪贞祐四年十二月髙琪请修南京里城宣宗有民力已困此役一兴病滋甚矣云云髙琪所载亦同似不行其议者完顔萨布兴定元年七月上章言外城虽坚然周六十余里仓猝有警难于拒守城中有子城故基宜于农隙筑而新之为国家久长之利从之其称外城周六十余里与此志八十里有异且在髙琪所请不行后半载上章与此志専指髙琪建议亦异然倡之者髙琪究以此志为得其实

    Explanation

    107

    Records of Returning to Obscurity, Volume Six
    <Novelists, Miscellaneous, Records of Returning to Obscurity>
    Imperially Approved Complete Library of the Four Treasuries

    Records of Returning to Obscurity, Volume Seven
    Yuan Dynasty, Liu Qi, compiled

    In the early years of the Xingding era, Zhu Ge Gaoqi, serving as chancellor, proposed the idea of dividing the city of Kaifeng, which had a circumference of eighty li, into an inner and outer city. The inner city, with a circumference of forty li, was constructed, causing the demolition of many houses and the suffering of the people of Henan. The large-scale mobilization of labor made the people of Henan suffer. Officials from the imperial court supervised the labor, dividing and overseeing different areas, and if they fell behind, they were beaten. [According to the History of the Jin Dynasty, in the twelfth month of the fourth year of the Zhenyou era, Gaoqi requested the repair of the inner city of Kaifeng. Emperor Xuanzong of Jin stated that the people were already suffering, and this project would only exacerbate their plight. This is consistent with the account in Gaoqi's biography, which suggests that the proposal was not implemented. However, the Biography of Wanyan Sabu records that in July of the first year of the Xingding era, Sabu submitted a memorial stating that although the outer city was strong, it was sixty to seventy li in circumference, making it difficult to defend in an emergency. He suggested that the old foundations of the inner city should be rebuilt during the farming off-season for the long-term benefit of the state. The emperor agreed. The description of the outer city being sixty to seventy li in circumference differs from the eighty li mentioned here, and it is also noted that after Gaoqi's proposal was not implemented, Sabu's memorial specifically referred to Gaoqi's suggestion, indicating that Gaoqi was the one who initially proposed the idea, and this account is considered more accurate.]

    108

    】及北兵入河南朝议守子城或云一失外城则子城非我有遂止守外城外城故宋所筑土脉甚坚北兵攻之旬余竟不拔而新筑子城殊无用也嗟乎愚人之虑何如哉使天下郡邑俱失纵然独保一子城何以国也然子城初起时于地中得一石碣上有诗云瑞云灵气镇城东他日还应与北同嵗月迁移人事变却来此地再兴工亦有数云其字书宋人迄今犹在相国寺大梁城南五里号青城乃金国初尼玛哈驻军受宋二帝降处【案尼玛哈旧作粘罕今改】当时后妃皇族皆诣焉因尽俘而北天兴末末帝东迁崔立以城降北兵亦于青城下寨而后妃内族复诣此地多僇死亦可怪也

    108

    When the northern army entered Henan, the court decided to defend the inner city, but some said that if we lose the outer city, the inner city will no longer be ours. Thus, they decided to defend only the outer city. The outer city, built by the Song Dynasty with very solid earthworks, was attacked by the northern army for more than ten days but could not be captured. However, the newly built inner city proved to be of no use. Alas, how foolish are these considerations! If all the counties and cities are lost, how can one inner city alone save the country?

    However, when the inner city was first constructed, a stone tablet was found in the ground, on which was inscribed a poem: "The auspicious cloud and spiritual energy will stabilize the eastern part of the city, and one day it will be the same as the north. As time passes and people change, this place will be rebuilt again, and there is a certain number of times this will happen." The characters are similar to those of the Song Dynasty and are still there at Xiangguosi Temple, five miles south of Daliang City, known as Qingcheng, where Nima Ha initially stationed his troops and received the surrender of the two Song emperors.

    At that time, the empresses, princesses, and imperial family all came to this place, and they were all captured and taken north. During the end of the Tianxing era, the last emperor of the Southern Song Dynasty moved eastward, and Cui Li surrendered the city to the northern army. They also set up camp at Qingcheng, and the empresses and inner family members again came to this place, where many were killed, which is also strange.

    109

    南渡之后南京虽繁盛益増然近年屡有妖怪元光间白日虎入郑门又吏部中有狐跃出宫中亦有狐及狼又夜闻哭辇路每日暮乌鹊蔽天皆亡国之兆迄今为丘墟瓦砾哀哉
    　　南京同乐园故宋龙徳宫徽宗所修其间楼阁花石甚盛每春三月花发及五六月荷花开官纵百姓观虽未尝再増葺然景物如旧正大末北兵入河南京城作防守计官尽毁之其楼亭材大者为楼橹用其湖石皆凿为礮矣迄今皆废区壊址荒芜所存者独熙春一阁耳葢其阁皆沙木壁饰上下无土泥虽欲毁之不能世岂复有此良匠也
    　　宣宗喜刑法政尚威严故南渡之在位者多苛刻图克坦右丞思忠【案图克坦旧作徒单今改】好用麻椎击人号麻椎相公李运使特立友之号半截剑冯内翰璧叔献号马刘子后雷希顔为御史至蔡州得奸豪杖杀五百人又号雷半千又有完顔玛嘉楚富察约尔珠【案玛嘉楚旧作麻斤出富察约尔珠旧作蒲察咬住今改】皆以酷闻而富察和卓【案富察和卓旧作蒲察合住今改】王阿里李涣之徒胥吏中尤狡刻者也

    109

    After the Southern Migration, Nanjing became even more prosperous, but in recent years, there have been frequent strange and supernatural occurrences. During the Yuan Guang period, a tiger entered Zheng Gate during the day, and a fox jumped out of the Ministry of Personnel. There were also foxes and wolves in the palace, and at night, one could hear the cries of ghosts on the imperial road. Every evening, crows and magpies covered the sky, all of which were signs of a falling nation. Up to now, it has become ruins and rubble, a source of sorrow.

    The Tongle Garden in Nanjing was the Longde Palace of the former Song Dynasty, built by Emperor Huizong. It was adorned with many pavilions, towers, flowers, and stones. In spring, the flowers bloomed, and in late spring and early summer, the lotus flowers opened. Officials allowed the common people to view it, and although it was not rebuilt, the scenery remained as beautiful as before. In the end of the Zhengda period, northern troops entered the capital of the Southern Song Dynasty, and defensive measures were taken. Officials destroyed it completely, using the large materials from the towers and pavilions for fortifications, and chiseled the lake stones into cannons. Up to now, they are all abandoned areas with ruined sites and desolate landscapes, with only the Xichun Pavilion remaining. The pavilion was built with sandwood and wall decorations, without soil or mortar, and even if one wanted to destroy it, it could not be done. The world no longer has such skilled craftsmen.

    Emperor Xuanzong preferred strict laws and a stern government, so those in power after the Southern Migration were often harsh. Tuotan Youcheng Sizhong, a high-ranking official, liked to beat people with a mallet and was called the Mallet Chancellor. Li Yunshi Tezhi, who befriended him, was called the Half-Sword. Feng Neihan Bi Shuxian was called the Horse Liuzi. Lei Xiyang, a censor, beat and killed 500 ruffians in Caizhou and was called the Half-Thousand Lei. Wanyan Majia Chu, a high-ranking official, was known for his cruelty, as were Fuca Hezhuo, Wang Ali, and Li Huan, who were particularly cunning and harsh among the clerks.

    110

    宣宗后妃皆出微贱南渡人有云头巾王过道史白酒厐指三外戚家也王氏有成国夫人者宣宗后妃之姊末帝之姨奢侈尤甚权势薫天当涂者往往纳赂取媚积赀如山且出入宫掖无时度号自在夫人天兴改元末帝东迁崔立之变凡富贵之家捜括金银成国竟棰死又有平章政事完顔巴萨【案巴萨旧作白撒今改】以内族位将相尤奢僭尝起第西城如宫掖然其中婢妾百数皆衣缕金绮绣如宫人在尚书省恶堂食不适口以其家膳供然为将相无他材能徒以仪体为事从末帝东征方渡河督战遽劝上回奔睢阳众以其误国归罪请废末帝不得已下狱饿死人皆快之

    Explanation

    110

    The consorts and concubines of Emperor Xuanzong were all of humble origins. It is said that during the period of the southward migration, there were three prominent families of imperial relatives, known by the mnemonic "Wang with a cap, Li who passed the street, and Pang with white wine." Among the Wang family, there was a consort known as Chengguo Princess, who was the sister of Emperor Xuanzong's consorts and the aunt of the last emperor. She was extremely extravagant and wielded immense power, often receiving bribes and favors. Her wealth accumulated to an enormous extent, and she moved freely in and out of the palace without restraint, earning the nickname "Zizai Princess." During the reign of the last emperor, the era name was changed to Tianxing, and the capital was moved eastward. During the rebellion led by Cui Li, all wealthy families were searched for their gold and silver, and Chengguo Princess was ultimately beaten to death. There was also a high-ranking official named Wanganyan Basa, who held the position of Pingzhangzhengshi and was a member of the imperial family. He was particularly extravagant and arrogant, having built a mansion in the western city that resembled a palace. Inside, he had over a hundred concubines and maids, all dressed in golden-threaded silk and brocade, akin to those in the palace. Despite his position as a chancellor, he had no real talent or ability, focusing only on appearances and ceremonies. He accompanied the last emperor on an eastern campaign and, while crossing the river and supervising the battle, suddenly advised the emperor to retreat to Suizhou. The people blamed him for the country's misfortunes and requested the deposition of the last emperor. Eventually, the last emperor was deposed and executed, and the people were pleased with the outcome.

    111

    南渡之后为宰执者往往无恢复之谋上下同风止以茍安目前为乐凡有人言当改革则必以生事抑之每北兵压境则君臣相对泣下或殿上发叹吁已而敌退解严则大张具防饮黄阁中矣每相与议时事至甚危处辄罢散曰俟再议已而复然因循茍且竟至亡国南渡之后朝廷近侍以谄防成风每有四方菑异或民间疾苦将奏之必相谓曰恐圣上心困当时有人云今日恐心困后日大心困矣竟至败亡又在位者临事往往不肯分明可否相习低言缓语互推让号养相体吁相体果

    Explanation

    111

    After the Southern Migration, those who held high-ranking positions in the government often lacked plans for recovery. The upper and lower ranks shared the same风气 (wind), settling for temporary safety and seeking short-term peace as their primary concern. Whenever someone suggested reforms, they were always suppressed with the excuse of "creating trouble." When the northern troops advanced to the border, the emperor and his ministers would weep together, sometimes expressing sighs and lamentations in the palace. Once the enemy retreated and the alert was lifted, they would throw lavish parties in the Yellow Hall. Whenever they discussed very dangerous situations, they would immediately disperse, saying they would discuss again later, and then proceed in a perfunctory manner. This negligence and carelessness eventually led to the collapse of the state.

    After the Southern Migration, the attendants in the court's inner circle developed a trend of flattery and fear of offending. Whenever there were disasters and unusual events in various regions or the hardships of the common people, those who wanted to report them would always say, "We fear that the emperor's heart would be troubled." At the time, someone remarked, "Today we fear troubling the emperor's heart, but tomorrow we will greatly trouble it." This ultimately led to the downfall of the state. Moreover, those in power often hesitated to clearly state whether something was right or wrong when faced with matters. They habitually spoke in low, slow tones, mutually shying away from responsibility, claiming to care for each other's well-being but actually not doing so.

    112

    安在哉又宰执用人必先择无锋铓輭孰易制者曰恐生事故正人君子多不得用虽用亦未久遽退闲宰执如张左丞行言台谏官如陈司谏规许司谏古程雷御史皆不能终其任也【案以上二条金史完顔努森节引其语过半】南渡之后近侍之权尤重葢宣宗喜用其人为耳目以伺察百官故使其奉御辈采访民间号行路御史或得一二事即入奏之上因以责台官漏泄皆抵罪又方面之柄虽委将帅又差一奉御在军中号监战每临机制变多为所牵制辄遇敌先奔故其军多防败

    Explanation

    112

    Where are they? When it comes to the selection of officials, the emperor and his ministers invariably choose those who are mild, submissive, and easy to control, saying that they fear incidents will arise. As a result, upright and virtuous people are often not employed, and even when they are, they are quickly dismissed. For example, officials like Zhang Zuo, the Left Chancellor, and Teng Shijian, the Censor-in-Chief, were unable to complete their duties. [Note: The above two points are quoted from the History of the Jin Dynasty, the biography of Wanyan Nusen, and the text exceeds half of the original.] After the Southern Migration, the power of those close to the emperor became especially significant. Emperor Xuanzong favored using such individuals as his eyes and ears to spy on and monitor the officials. Therefore, he sent his attendants to investigate the people, calling them "road inspectors." If they found one or two things, they immediately reported to the emperor, who then blamed the censors for leaking information, and they were all punished. Moreover, the power over regional administration was entrusted to military commanders, but another attendant was sent to the army, called a "monitor of war." Whenever they faced changing circumstances and had to make decisions, they were often constrained and hindered, and when they encountered the enemy, they fled first, leading to frequent defeats in their military campaigns.

    113

    贞祐间珠赫髙琪为相欲树党固其权先擢用文人将以为羽翼已而台谏官许古刘元规之徒见其恣横相继言之髙琪大怒斥罢二人因此大恶进士更用胥吏彼喜其奬拔往往为尽心于是吏权大盛胜进士矣又髙琪定制省部寺监官防注进士吏员又使由郡转部由部转台省不三五年皆得要职士大夫反畏避其锋而宣宗亦喜此曹刻深故时全由小吏侍东宫至为佥枢宻院事南征帅又有富察和卓王阿里之徒居左右司李涣辈在外行尚书六部陷士夫数十人亦亡国之政也

    Explanation

    113

    During the reign of Emperor Shizong in the Jurchen Jin dynasty, Gao Qi, as chancellor, sought to build a faction to consolidate his power by first promoting literati, whom he intended to use as his allies. However, officials such as Xu Gu and Liu Yuan Gui, who were responsible for oversight and remonstration, saw his arrogance and domineering behavior and spoke out against him. Gao Qi became furious and dismissed them, which led to a great detestation of the scholar-officials. He then promoted low-ranking officials, who were pleased with their elevation and often devoted themselves fully to his cause. Consequently, the power of these officials greatly increased, surpassing that of the scholar-officials. Gao Qi also established a policy to appoint both scholar-officials and low-ranking officials to various central government departments. He further arranged for these officials to rise from local to central government positions, and from there to the highest administrative bodies, achieving important positions within three to five years. Scholar-officials, in turn, feared and avoided their influence. Emperor Xuanzong also favored these officials for their rigorous and strict policies. Thus, it became common for low-ranking officials to serve in the imperial palace, even reaching positions such as vice-president of the Military Council and generals leading southern campaigns. Additionally, officials like Fu Cha He Zhuo Wang Ali and others held key positions in the central government departments, while Li Huan and his peers implemented the Six Ministries of the Shangshu Province, ensnaring dozens of scholar-officials. This was indeed a policy of a collapsing state.

    114

    南渡后屡兴师伐宋葢其意以河南陜西狭隘将取地南中夫已所有不能保而夺人所有岂有是理然连年征伐亦未尝大有功虽破蕲黄杀虏良多较其士马物故且屡为水陷溺亦相当也最后盱眙军改为镇淮府以军戍之费粮数万未防亦弃云又师还乗夏多刈熟麦以归助军储故宋人边檄有云暴卒鸱张率作如林之旅饥氓乌合驱归得罪之人驸马都尉布萨阿哈【案布萨阿哈旧作仆散阿海今改】佥枢宻院事时全皆回辕即诛后又谋取蜀时胥平章鼎镇闗中奏请缓发胥由此罢相嗟乎避强欺弱望其复振难哉此皆宣宗时事末帝即位无南伐之议矣

    Explanation

    114

    After crossing south, the Jin dynasty repeatedly launched military campaigns against the Song dynasty. The purpose was to make Henan and Shaanxi provinces narrow, intending to take control of the southern territories. However, taking what others have is not reasonable. Year after year of military campaigns did not result in significant success. Although many enemy soldiers were killed in the battles of Qihuang, when comparing the loss of soldiers, horses, and equipment, and the frequent sinking due to water, it is about equal. Finally, the army at Xuyi was changed to the Zhenhuifu, with an army garrisoned there, costing tens of thousands of grains of rice. Without defense, it was also abandoned. When the army returned, they took advantage of the summer to harvest mature wheat to help replenish the military supplies. Therefore, the Song people's border edict said: "Rampaging soldiers, like crows spreading their wings, form a forest-like force, hungry and disorganized, driven back as guilty ones." Prince Consort and Vice Marshal Busa Aha (formerly known as Puxian Aihai, now changed) served as the Deputy Chancellor of the Privy Council. Upon his return to his camp, he was immediately executed. Later, when planning to capture Sichuan, Xushang Pingzhang Dǐng, who was stationed in Guanzhong, requested a delay, and Xushang was thus dismissed from his position. Alas, avoiding the strong and bullying the weak, hoping for a revival is difficult. These were all events during the reign of Emperor Xuanzong. When the last emperor ascended the throne, there was no discussion of a southern campaign.

    115

    甚哉风俗之移人也南渡后吏权大盛自髙琪为相定法其迁转与进士等甚者反疾焉故一时之人争以此进虽士大夫家有子弟读书往往不终辄令改试台部令史其子弟辈既习此业便与进士为仇其趋进举止全学吏曹至有舞文纳赂甚于吏辈者惟侥幸一时进用不顾平日源流可为长太息者也
    　　金朝取士止以词赋经义学士大夫往往局于此不能多读书其格法最陋者词赋状元即授应奉翰林文字不问其人材何如故多有不任其事者或顾问不称上意被笑嗤出补外官章宗时王状元泽在翰林防宋使进枇杷子上索诗泽奏小臣不识枇杷子惟王庭筠诗成上喜之吕状元造父子魁多士及在翰林上索重阳诗造素不学诗惶遽献诗云佳节近重阳微臣喜欲狂上大笑旋令外补故当时有云泽民不识枇杷子吕造能吟喜欲狂

    Explanation

    115

    How powerful is the influence of customs on people! After the southward crossing, the power of officials greatly increased, and when Gao Qi became chancellor, he established rules that made their promotions equal to those of Jinshi (imperial scholars), and in some cases, even faster. Thus, people at the time competed to advance through this path, and even noble families often made their sons stop reading and instead sent them to take exams for administrative positions. Once these sons entered this profession, they became enemies of the Jinshi, and their behavior and mannerisms were entirely modeled after bureaucrats. Some even manipulated legal documents and accepted bribes more than bureaucrats themselves. They only hoped for temporary advancement without considering their past origins, which is truly a cause for long sighs.

    In the Jin dynasty, officials were selected solely based on poetry, prose, and Confucian classics. Scholars often became limited to this, unable to read much more. The most superficial of these selection methods was that the top scorer in poetry and prose was immediately appointed as a scholar in the imperial academy, regardless of the quality of the person. As a result, many were not fit for their roles, and some, when questioned by the emperor, failed to meet his expectations and were laughed at and demoted to local positions. During the reign of Emperor Zhangzong, Wang Zhe, a Jinshi, was in the imperial academy when a Song envoy brought loquat fruit. The emperor asked for a poem, and Wang replied that he did not recognize the loquat fruit. Only after Wang Tingyun's poem was completed did the emperor show satisfaction. Similarly, when Emperor Zhangzong asked for a Double Ninth Festival poem, Liu Zao, another Jinshi, was unaccustomed to writing poetry and hastily composed one, saying, "On this festival near Double Ninth, I am so delighted that I am on the verge of madness." The emperor laughed and soon ordered him to be reassigned to a local position. Thus, at the time, it was said, "Wang Zhe did not recognize the loquat fruit, and Liu Zao could recite poetry with great enthusiasm."

    116

    兴定初朝议县令最亲民依常调数换多不得人始诏内外七品以上官保举仍升为正七品【案金史选举志兴定元年令随朝七品外路六品以上职事官举正七品以下职事官云云则内七品外六品始为举主与此志内外七品以上官均得保举异又攷宣宗本纪兴定三年十一月辛丑诏朝官七品外路六品以上二嵗举县令一人而兴定元年无此事与选举志亦异】资未及者借注人一时能吏如王登庸令洛阳【案金史循吏云偃师王登庸与此异】程
    震威卿令陈留皆有治绩或入为监察御史台部官自是居官者争以能相尚民亦多受赐其后往往由纳赂请托以得之故疲懦贪者亦多然士大夫为之者犹自力此良法也哉正大初末帝鋭于政朝议置益政院官院居宫中选一时宿望有学者如杨学士云翼史修撰公燮吕待制造数人兼之轮直每日朝罢侍上讲尚书贞观政要数篇间亦及民间事有补益杨公又与赵学士秉文共集自古治术分门号君臣政要为一编进之此亦开讲学之渐也然嵗余亦罢

    Explanation

    116

    In the early years of the Xingding era, the court discussed that county magistrates are closest to the people and that according to the usual rotation system, they were frequently changed, often resulting in unsuitable officials. The court then issued an edict requiring officials of the seventh rank or higher to recommend candidates and promoted them to the seventh rank. Those who did not meet the qualifications were temporarily appointed. At that time, capable officials like Wang Dengyong served as magistrates in Luoyang and Cheng Zhenwei served as magistrate in Chenliu, all of whom had good governance records. Some were promoted to positions in the Censorate or the Six Ministries. From then on, officials competed to be competent, and the people also benefited greatly. Later, many positions were obtained through bribery and favoritism, so there were also many weak, lazy, and greedy officials. However, those who were scholars and officials still exerted themselves. This was indeed a good law. In the early years of the Zhengda era, the last emperor was keen on politics, and the court discussed setting up officials in the Office of Beneficial Politics. The office was located in the palace, and officials from the most respected and learned scholars, such as Yang Xueshi, Yun Yi, Shi Xiu, Gong Xie, and Lu Daimao, were selected to serve in the office. They took turns to serve daily after court sessions, advising the emperor on the Shangshu Zhen Guan Zhengyao (Essential Points of Governance from the Zhenguan Era) and other texts, occasionally discussing matters of the people that were beneficial. Yang Xueshi also compiled with Zhao Xueshi a collection of political strategies from ancient times, titled Jun Chen Zhengyao (Essential Points of Ruler and Minister), and presented it to the emperor. This also marked the beginning of scholarly discussions. However, after a year, it was abolished.

    117

    士气不可不素养如明昌泰和间崇文养士故一时士大夫争以敢言敢为相尚迨大安中北兵入境往往以节死如王晦髙子约梁询谊诸人皆有名而侯挚李英田琢辈皆由下位自奋于兵间虽功业不成其志气有可嘉者南渡后宣宗奬用胥吏抑士大夫凡有敢为敢言者多被斥逐故一时在位者多委靡惟求免罪罟茍容迨天兴之变士大夫无一人死节者岂非有以致之欤由是言之士气不可不素养也
    　　南渡后疆土狭隘止河南陜西故仕进调官皆不得遽入仕或守十余载号重复累往往归耕或教小学养生故当时有云古人谓十年牕下无人问一举成名天下知今日一举成名天下知十年牕下无人问也其后有辟举法行虽未入仕亦得辟为令故新进士多便得一邑治民其省令史亦以次召补故士人方免沈滞之叹云

    Explanation

    117

    The spirit of scholars cannot be neglected in cultivation. During the Mingchang and Taihe periods, the value of literature and the nurturing of scholars led to a time when scholars competed to be bold in speech and decisive in action. By the Da'an period, when northern troops entered the country, many scholars died with integrity, such as Wang Hui, Gao Ziyue, Liang Xunyi, and others, who were all famous. Meanwhile, Hou Zhi, Li Ying, and Tian Zhuo, among others, rose up in the midst of war, even though their achievements were not realized, their spirit and courage are commendable. After the Southern Migration, Emperor Xuan favored the use of胥吏 (low-ranking officials) and suppressed the gentry and officials. Those who dared to speak and act were often dismissed, so those in power became listless and weak, seeking only to avoid punishment and leniency. By the time of the Tianxing upheaval, not a single scholar died with integrity. Is this not due to this reason? Therefore, the spirit of scholars cannot be neglected in cultivation.

    After the Southern Migration, the territory was narrow, limited to Henan and Shaanxi. Officials and appointments could not be made hastily; one might serve for over ten years, suffering from repeated burdens, and often return to farming or teach in elementary schools, cultivating oneself. At the time, it was said, "In ancient times, ten years of study in a windowless room went unnoticed, but one success brought fame throughout the land. Today, one success brings fame throughout the land, but ten years of study in a windowless room goes unnoticed." Later, the system of appointment by recommendation was implemented, so even those who had not entered officialdom could be appointed as county magistrates. Thus, newly graduated scholars often immediately governed a county, and provincial clerks were also summoned and appointed in turn. Therefore, scholars were finally relieved of the sigh of being stuck.

    118

    大臣尤当以至公至正黜陟百官大不可畏嫌避党为自保计南渡为宰执者多怯惧畏懦不敢有为凡处一事先恐人疑已如宰执本进士或士大夫得罪知其无辜不敢辨言恐人疑其为党也又或转加诘责以示无私或要职美官宁用他人取媚于众一登省府遽忘本来用心如此望其成功名立节义难矣然亦往往不能以富贵自终向使以公正自持未必以是得罪也人云用智巧者竟何如哉
    　　宰相之职佐人主治天下所患耳目不广不能周知民间苦乐国势安危故当忘私去智取诸人以为善以天下治天下至于百官士流贤否皆当如家人美恶一一辨其才然后进退用舍合公望办职业而为国者立法使百官宾客不得谒见于私第何哉其意正以防其请托而徇私也夫果察其人徇私不公岂可使为宰相哉既以为宰相是已

    Explanation

    118

    High-ranking officials should evaluate and promote or demote officials based on utmost fairness and righteousness. They should not fear and avoid party affiliations out of self-preservation. After the Southern Migration, many prime ministers were cowardly and timid, unwilling to take action. In handling any matter, they first feared that others might suspect them. For example, officials who were originally scholars or gentlemen, if wrongfully accused and knew they were innocent, dared not defend themselves, fearing that others might suspect them of being part of a party. Sometimes, they were further questioned and criticized to show impartiality, or they preferred to give important positions to others to curry favor with the masses. Once they entered high office, they quickly forgot their original intentions and principles. It is difficult to expect them to achieve success, establish a reputation, and uphold righteousness. Yet, they often cannot end their lives in wealth and honor. If they had maintained fairness and integrity, they would not have been wrongfully accused. People say that those who use cunning and cleverness will ultimately be what?

    The role of the chancellor is to assist the ruler in governing the country, but the problem is that they do not have wide-ranging vision and cannot fully understand the hardships and joys of the people or the security and danger of the nation. Therefore, they should forget their personal interests and wisdom, and learn from others to do good and govern the country with the people. Regarding the officials and scholars, whether they are virtuous or not, they should be treated like family members, and their talents should be evaluated one by one, and then they should be promoted, demoted, retained, or dismissed based on public opinion and their duties. The ruler should also establish laws to prevent officials and guests from visiting privately. Why is this so? It is to prevent them from making requests and acting out of private interests. If one truly observes that someone acts out of private interests and is unfair, how can they be made a chancellor? If one has already been made a chancellor, then it is so.

    119

    以天下付之矣诚不宜犹尔防闲也唐裴晋公一日拜相遽请于私第见百官宾客可谓逺谋而宪宗信之卒平淮蔡此其君臣遇合故有此竒伟士成功名使龊龊者为之亦不敢请而庸主亦不聴也余观南渡后为宰执者自非亲戚故旧往往不得登其门若夫百官士流未尝接议论局局自保惟恐失之如此望其所用得人闻见不塞者未之有也
    　　士大夫为吏者当以至公无心处之事自理民自服不可委曲要誉以枉义也余在南方时见辟举为令者往往妄用心如富家与贫家讼必直贫民势家与百姓争必直百姓不问埋何如也又或故旧同道之家有科征必先督促不少贷至加之刑罚其意以为如此示吾无私且贾细民称誉嗟乎贫富相争自有曲直彼贫民中亦有

    Explanation

    119

    The world has been entrusted to him, but it is truly inappropriate to still be so cautious. During the Tang Dynasty, Pei Ji, upon being appointed as chancellor, suddenly requested to meet officials and guests at his private residence, showing a bold and unexpected move. This was a distant plan, and Emperor Xianzong trusted him, leading to the final pacification of Huai and Cai. This is the meeting of the ruler and minister, hence this extraordinary person achieved success and fame. Even if someone petty were to do it, they would not dare to ask, and a mediocre ruler would not listen. After the Southern Migration, those who became chancellors, unless they were relatives or old friends, often could not enter their doors. Officials and scholars never engage in discussions, each protecting themselves, fearing loss so much. Those who hope to use capable people and have unobstructed knowledge have never existed.

    As for officials, they should handle affairs with utmost fairness and impartiality, and the people will naturally submit. One should not twist things to gain praise at the expense of justice. When I was in the south, I saw those appointed as magistrates often act recklessly. When a wealthy family sues a poor family, the poor are always right; when a powerful family sues a commoner, the commoner is always right; the commoner does not care about the outcome. Or when there are old friends and fellow travelers, they always demand payment first and do not hesitate to impose penalties. They think this shows they are impartial and gain praise from the common people, but alas, in disputes between rich and poor, there is always right and wrong, and among the poor, there are also distinctions.

    120

    桀不逞者富家者亦有循良懦弱者乌可执一哉故同道之家义当假借不然止以无心处之可也至首加讯责不亦伤乎大抵此曹志于升进故尔甚者榜于门云无亲戚故旧不见宾客不接士人世岂有一为郡邑而遽无亲无旧者尝记有一人为县令禁其子不令出其子犯禁笞责之其子赴井死哀哉不循中道纵得升迁何荣也
    　　国所以设官士所以居官先以养其口体妻子然后得専意王事虽不可取于民奢纵害公亦不必钓名要誉太俭陋也余见河南为令者夜葢纸被朝服敝衣以示亷又令妻子辈汲爨不使吏卒代者其意皆欲闻上位媚细人然其聴断抚养之道殊不在是能使其车骑仪从屋宇服用整鲜而遇事风生吏民称快较之此曹何自苦也

    Explanation

    120

    A person of bad character or discontent, and those who are wealthy but also virtuous and well-behaved, or weak-willed and timid—how can one adhere to one principle? Therefore, those who share the same path should extend leniency. Otherwise, one can only deal with them innocently. To immediately interrogate and blame them would not be harmful? Generally, these people intend to advance in rank, so they post notices on their doors claiming they have no relatives or old friends, do not receive guests, and do not associate with scholars. Is it possible for someone to suddenly have no relatives or old friends when serving as a magistrate? I once saw a person who served as a magistrate, forbidding his son from going out; his son violated the prohibition and was beaten, leading to his suicide by jumping into a well. Alas! Not following the middle way, even if one obtains promotion, what honor is there?

    The state establishes officials, and scholars hold office, first to support their basic needs and families, then to devote themselves to state affairs. Though it is not possible to take from the people, excessive luxury harms the public, and one does not necessarily need to seek fame or reputation, as being too frugal can be vulgar. I have seen magistrates in Henan who cover themselves with paper quilts at night and wear tattered official robes in the morning to show frugality. They also make their wives and children draw water and cook, not allowing officials or soldiers to substitute. Their intention is all to be heard by their superiors and flatter the common people. However, their methods of judgment and nurturing are not in this. They can make their chariots, horses, and ceremonial attendants neat and fresh, and handle matters efficiently, earning praise from officials and common people. Compared to these people, how self-torturing is it?

    121

    南渡后士风甚薄一登仕籍视布衣诸生遽为两途至于征逐游从辄相分别故布衣有事或数谒见在位者在位者相报复甚希甚者髙居台阁旧交不得见故李长源愤其如此尝曰以区区一第傲天下士耶已第者闻之多怒至逐长源出史院又交讼于官士风如此可叹
    　　省吏前朝止用胥吏号堂后官金朝大定初张太师浩制皇家袒免亲宰执子试补外杂用进士凡登第厯三任至县令以次召补充一攷三十月出得六品州倅两攷六十月得五品节度副使留守判官或就选为知除知案由之以渐得都事左右司员外郎郎中【案洪皓松漠纪闻省部有令史以进士及第者为之葢纪天眷皇统间事又金史王蔚皇统二年进士由良乡丞补尚书省令史洊迁都事左司员外郎郎中大定二年超授转运使则以进士为尚书省令史不始自大定初金史选举志云省令史正隆元年止于宻院台及六部吏人令史内选充大定二年罢吏人而复皇统选进士之制葢即

    Explanation

    121

    After the Southern Crossing, the scholarly风气 was very thin. Once they entered officialdom, they treated commoners and students as two different paths. In matters of pursuing and associating with others, they immediately distinguished themselves. Commoners with matters would often visit those in power, but those in power rarely reciprocated. Especially those who held high positions did not even see old friends. Li Changyuan was angry about this and once said, "To be proud of just passing the imperial exams and look down on scholars all over the world?" Those who had passed the exams heard this and were often angry, to the point of expelling Li Changyuan from the history bureau and also filing lawsuits against him in court. The scholarly风气 is like this, it is truly pitiful.

    In the early days, only clerks were used in the provincial administration, known as "tanghou guan." During the early Dading period of the Jin Dynasty, Zhang Taishi Hao established a system where royal relatives and high officials' sons were tested and appointed as external officials, using Jinshi (imperial exam graduates). All who passed the exams and served three terms as county magistrates were successively summoned and supplemented. After one exam, they could serve for thirty months and become a sixth-rank state deputy. After two exams, they could serve for sixty months and become a fifth-rank deputy governor or a deputy governor of a capital city. They could also choose to serve as a judge or a case handler and gradually become a deputy secretary, an assistant to the left or right, or a director. According to Hong Hao's Songmo Jiwu, the provincial administration had clerks who were Jinshi graduates, which recorded events during the Tianjuan and Huangtong periods. According to the Jin Shi biography of Wang Wei, in the second year of Huangtong, he passed the Jinshi exams and was appointed as a clerk in the Ministry of Works, then promoted to various positions. In the second year of Dading, he was appointed as a transport commissioner. Thus, Jinshi graduates became clerks in the Ministry of Works, not starting from the early Dading period. The Jin Shi election records state that in the first year of Zhenglong, clerks were selected from the Secretariat and the Six Ministries, and in the second year of Dading, the selection of clerks from the Six Ministries was abolished and the system of selecting Jinshi graduates was reinstated.

    122

    张浩所议复者此志云前朝止用胥吏竟似大定之前未有用进士者失之疎漏当与金史防证所云登第厯三任至县令以次召补充似由进士厯三任为县令其语意亦欠分明攷金史贺庭由安肃令补尚书省令史元徳明子好问由南阳令擢尚书省掾皆不详其厯三任为县令萧贡由泾州观察判官补尚书省令史路伯达由泗州使补尚书省掾冯璧自东阿丞召补尚书省令史皆非由县令召补则或三任或县令未尝并为一也至所云一攷出得六品州倅两考得五品节度副使等官者亦张浩定议如是后来不皆循其制李献甫以尚书省令史充行六部员外郎元好问以省

    Explanation

    122

    Zhang Hao's proposed reforms are recorded here, stating that the previous administration only used clerks, seemingly implying that before the Da Ding period, there were no scholars (jinshi). This is a lack of detail and should be verified against the History of Jin. The text mentions that those who passed the imperial examination and served three terms before becoming a county magistrate were subsequently appointed. It seems to suggest that scholars who served three terms became county magistrates, but the meaning is not entirely clear. Examining the History of Jin, it is noted that He Yangting, after serving as the magistrate of Anshu, was appointed as a clerk in the Shangshu Province. Yuan Deming's son, Yuan Hao, after serving as the magistrate of Nanyang, was promoted to an assistant in the Shangshu Province. Neither of these accounts details his three terms as a county magistrate. Xiao Gong, after serving as an observer judge in Jingzhou, was appointed as a clerk in the Shangshu Province. Lu Bodu, after serving as an envoy in Sizhou, was appointed as an assistant in the Shangshu Province. Feng Bi, after serving as a deputy magistrate in Dong'e, was summoned and appointed as a clerk in the Shangshu Province. None of these individuals were appointed from the position of county magistrate, indicating that either they served three terms or they were appointed as county magistrates, but not both. Regarding the statement that one examination led to the position of a sixth-rank state deputy governor and two examinations led to the position of a fifth-rank deputy governor of a military region, this was also proposed by Zhang Hao. However, not all followed this pattern. For instance, Li Xianfu, after serving as a clerk in the Shangshu Province, was appointed as an acting deputy minister of the six departments. Yuan Hao, after serving as a clerk in the Shangshu Province, was appointed as an acting deputy minister of the six departments.

    123

    掾除左司都事萧贡以令史擢监察御史或疑本有省文然果出为州倅及节度副使等官自不缺载如贺庭以令史授泌南军节度副使入为监察御史路伯达以省掾除兴平军节度副使入为大理司直固备详之矣又杨伯渊天防初以名家子补尚书省令史十四年赐进士第是天眷皇统以前有先为令史后第进士者与举志散阶赴防试之制相合益征省令史之専用胥吏惟正隆时耳】故仕进者以此途为捷径如不为省令史即循资级得五品甚迟故有节察令推何日了盐度户勾防时休之语浩初定制时语人曰省庭天下仪表如用胥吏定行货赂混淆用进士清源也其进士

    Explanation

    123

    Xiao Gong was appointed as the Left Department Director of the Left Office, promoted from a clerical position to an inspector of the imperial court. Some may doubt that the original text has been abridged, but indeed, he was appointed as a deputy governor of a state and as a deputy military governor, etc., and these appointments are not omitted in the records. For example, He Yangting was appointed as a deputy military governor of the Bi Nan army as a clerk, and later became an inspector of the imperial court. Lu Boduo was appointed as a deputy military governor of the Xingping army as a clerk in the Shangshu Province, and later became a justice of the Dali Court. These appointments are indeed detailed in the records. Additionally, in the biography of Yang Boyuan, it is recorded that in the early days of Tianfang, a son of a prominent family was appointed as a clerk in the Shangshu Province, and in the fourteenth year, he was granted the degree of Jinshi. This shows that before the reigns of Tianjuan and Huangtong, there were cases of people first serving as clerks and later passing the Jinshi examination, which is consistent with the system of promotion through examinations for commoners. This further proves that the exclusive use of clerks in the Shangshu Province was only during the Zhenglong period. Thus, those seeking official careers considered this path the quickest way. If one does not serve as a clerk in the Shangshu Province, it takes a long time to reach the fifth rank through regular promotions. Thus, there were sayings like "When will the inspector and the clerk finish their duties? When will the salt and grain officials rest?" When Hao first established the regulations, he told people, "The Shangshu Province is the model of the empire; if clerks are used, corruption will prevail; if Jinshi are used, the source will be clear."

    124

    受赇如良家子女犯奸也胥吏公亷如娼女守节也议者皆以为当屏山尝为余言之然省令史仪礼冠带抱书进趋与掾吏不殊有过辄决杖惜乎以胥吏待天下士也故士大夫有气概者往往不就如雷翰林希顔魏翰林彦宋翰林飞卿及余先子或召补不愿或暂为遽告出皆不能终其任也李丈钦止为余言宋制省曹有检正皆士大夫其堂吏主行移文书也且问余以宋制与金制孰优余以为宋制善钦止曰此议与吾合也金朝用人大概由省令史左右司郎中贠外郎首领官取其簿书精干也由左右首领官选宰相执政取其奏对详敏也其经济大略安在哉此所以在位者多长于吏事也

    Explanation

    124

    Accepting bribes is like a virtuous family's daughter committing adultery, and the integrity of clerks is like a courtesan observing chastity. Scholars all believe that this matter should be discussed, and I have heard it mentioned by Mr. Ping Shan. However, the clerks in the government departments wear official robes and carry books, advancing and retreating like other officials. When they make mistakes, they are punished with beatings, which is regrettable, as clerks are treated as if they were common officials. Therefore, scholars with bold spirit often refuse to take office, such as Lei Hanying, Wei Hanlin, Song Hanlin Fei Qing, and my father. Some were summoned to fill positions but did not wish to accept them, or they took office temporarily and then resigned quickly, all of whom could not complete their terms.

    Mr. Li Qinzhi told me that in the Song dynasty, the government departments had officials called "jianzheng," who were all scholars. The clerks in the hall were responsible for handling official documents. He also asked me which system was better, the Song or the Jin. I replied that the Song system was better. Mr. Li agreed, saying that this view matched his own.

    In the Jin dynasty, officials were generally selected from clerks in the government departments, and from the leaders of the left and right offices, who were chosen for their administrative skills. High-ranking officials were selected by the leaders of the left and right offices, who were chosen for their detailed and prompt responses to imperial inquiries. However, where was the overall economic strategy? This is why those in power were skilled in administrative matters but lacked broader economic vision.

    125

    金朝兵制最弊每有征伐或邉衅动下令签军州县骚动其民家有数丁男好身手成时尽拣取无遗号泣怨嗟国家以为苦驱此辈战欲其克胜艰哉贞祐初下令签军防一时任子为监官者以春赴吏部调数宰执使尽防取号监官军其人愤愠号叫交愬于台省又冲宰相卤簿告丞相布萨齐勤【案旧作仆散七斤今改】大怒趣左右取弓矢射去已而上知其不可用免之元光末备潼闗黄河又下令签军诸使者厯郡邑自见居官者外无文武大
    小执事官皆拣之至许州前戸部郎中侍御史刘元规年防六十亦中选为千戸至陈州予先子以前监察御史亦为千户自余不可胜言既立部曲须依军例以次相钤束物议喧然后亦罢之嗟乎以任子为兵已失体况以朝士大夫充斯役乎当是时余以终塲举人获免而先子以御史不免立法之以至于斯余赴试开封先子以诗送之且寄赵闲闲雷希顔有云老作一兵吾命也芳聮七桂汝身之厚禄故人如见问为言尘土困王尼二公览之一笑

    Explanation

    125

    The military system of the Jin Dynasty was most flawed; every time there was a campaign or border conflict, an order was issued to conscript soldiers, causing unrest in the counties and towns. When a family had several strong men, they were all chosen without leaving any behind, leading to crying and lamenting among the people. The state considered it a burden to force these men to fight, hoping they would win, but it was difficult. In the early years of the Zhenyou era, an order was issued to conscript soldiers, and those who were temporarily appointed as supervisors were required to go to the Ministry of Personnel in spring for adjustments. The chief officials ordered them to be fully conscripted as supervisor soldiers. These people were angry, shouted, and complained to the central government, rushing through the prime minister's entourage to report to Chancellor Pu Sa Qi Qin. He became very angry and ordered his attendants to shoot arrows at them. Later, the emperor realized they were not useful and dismissed them. In the late years of the Yuanguang era, preparations were made for the defense of Tongguan and the Yellow River, and an order was issued to conscript soldiers. Various envoys visited the counties and towns, conscripting all officials, whether civil or military, except those who were already in office. In Xuzhou, the former Minister of Finance and Censor Liu Yuan Gui, who was sixty years old, was also selected as a thousand-household commander. In Chenzhou, my father, who was previously an Inspector, was also made a thousand-household commander. Once the units were established, they had to follow military regulations and be controlled in order. After much public criticism, it was eventually abolished. Alas, conscripting officials as soldiers has already lost its dignity, let alone conscripting court officials. At that time, I was exempted as a final candidate, but my father, who was an Inspector, was not. The flaws in the law led to this. I went to Kaifeng for the exam, and my father sent me a poem to accompany me, also sending it to Zhao Xianxian and Lei Xiyang, saying, "Old man, I am a soldier, it is my fate. You have a thick salary, seven good connections, and if old friends ask, tell them I am trapped in the dust like Wang Ni." The two gentlemen read it and smiled.

    126

    金朝近习之权甚重置近侍局于宫中职虽五品其要密与宰相等如旧日中书故多以资戚世家恩幸者居其职士大夫不预焉【案金史百官志近侍局提防正五品使从五品副使从六品直长正八品此志云职五品葢独据提防及局使而言之也其除授局职多以优等之官金史完顔苏兰以监察御史擢近侍局直长以正七品之台官转正八品之直长仍云擢则其体特优于常秩可知又提防局使虽止属五品而兼此职者亦多用优等之官内族庆善努贞祜初迁武卫军副都指挥使兼提防近侍局则以正四品兼之完顔匡匡以秘书监及签书枢密院事兼近侍局则以正三品兼之其不兼此职者独宰执耳】南渡后人主尤委任

    Explanation

    126

    The power of the close attendants in the Jin Dynasty was very significant, and the Near Attendants Bureau was established within the palace. Although the rank was fifth grade, its importance and confidentiality were equal to that of the chancellor, similar to the old Secretariat. Therefore, these positions were often filled by those who were favored due to their family connections or personal favors, and scholars and officials were not involved in them. According to the "Official Positions" chapter in the Jin History, the Near Attendants Bureau had a Director of Inspection at the fifth grade, an Assistant Director at the sixth grade, and a Supervisor at the eighth grade. This record mentions the position as fifth grade, which is only referring to the Director of Inspection and the Bureau Director. The appointment of positions in the bureau often involved higher-ranking officials. According to the biography of Wanyan Sulan in the Jin History, he was promoted from a seventh-grade inspector to an eighth-grade supervisor in the Near Attendants Bureau, indicating that such positions were particularly esteemed. Although the Director of Inspection and the Bureau Director were only fifth grade, those who held these positions alongside others were often higher-ranking officials. According to the biography of Qing Shannu in the Inner Clan, he was promoted to Deputy Commander of the Imperial Guard and concurrently served as Director of Inspection in the Near Attendants Bureau, which was a fourth-grade position. According to the biography of Wanyan Kuang, he served as Secretary and concurrently as Director of the Near Attendants Bureau, which was a third-grade position. Those who did not hold this position were only the heads of the government. After the southern migration, the emperor especially entrusted them with important duties.

    127

    大抵视宰执台部官皆外人而所谓腹心则此局也其局官以下所谓奉御奉职辈本以防诏供使令而人主委信反在士大夫右故大臣要官往往曲意奉承或被命出外帅臣郡守百计馆馈葢以其亲近易得言也然此曹皆膏粱子弟惟以妆饰体様相夸膏面镊须鞌马衣服鲜整朝夕侍上迎合谄媚以逸乐尊人主安其身又沮壊正人招贿赂为不法至于大臣退黜百官得罪多自局中御史之权反在其下矣其后欲外望颇杂用士人完顔伯居之不嵗余亦罢又于台部令史选奉职数人又于进士中亦选一二人充备其人既入局中则趋进举止曽亦未闻有正言补益者且此曹本仆役之职士大夫处之可羞而一二子泰然自以为荣亦陋也

    127

    Generally speaking, the high-ranking officials of the government and the censorate are considered outsiders, while those in this particular office are regarded as the inner circle of trusted advisors. The officials within this office, including the attendants and those responsible for duties, are primarily tasked with delivering imperial edicts and serving the emperor's commands. However, the emperor places his trust in them, often placing them above the scholar-officials. As a result, high-ranking officials often flatter and curry favor with them, and when sent on missions, they are entertained by military commanders and prefectural governors, as they are easy to approach and speak with.

    However, these officials are often the offspring of wealthy and noble families, who focus on their appearance and luxury, boasting about their elaborate grooming and fashionable attire. They serve the emperor day and night, flattering and fawning to secure their positions and enjoy leisure and pleasure, thereby honoring the emperor and securing their own status. They also undermine upright officials, accept bribes, and engage in illegal conduct. As a result, many high-ranking officials are demoted or dismissed, and the censors, who are supposed to monitor and report on the conduct of officials, often have less power than these officials.

    Later, to broaden the reputation and influence of the office, scholars were also employed alongside other officials. For example, Wang Anshi (a historical figure) served in this office for a few years before being dismissed. Additionally, several clerks from the censorate were selected to serve in this office, and a few graduates of the imperial examinations were also chosen to supplement the ranks. However, once these individuals entered the office, their manners and conduct did not seem to contribute positively, and they did not speak frankly or offer constructive advice. Moreover, these officials are essentially servants or menial workers, and it is shameful for scholar-officials to serve in such a capacity. Yet, some of these individuals feel proud and unconcerned about their low status, which is also vulgar and base.

    128

    归潜志卷七
    　　钦定四库全书
    　　归潜志卷八
    　　元　刘祁　撰
    　　金朝取士以词赋为重故士人往往不暇读书为他文尝闻先进故老见子弟辈读蘓黄诗辄怒斥故学者止工于律赋问之他文则懵然不知间有登第后始读书为文者诸名士是也南渡以来士人多为古学以着文作诗相髙然旧日专为科举之学者疾之为仇雠苦分为两途互相诋讥其作诗文者目举子为科举之学为科举之学者指文士为任子弟笑其不工科举殊不知国家敕设科举用四篇文字本取全才盖赋以择制诰之才诗以取风骚之防策以究

    Explanation

    128

    Records of Returning to Obscurity, Volume Seven
    Imperially Approved Complete Library
    Records of Returning to Obscurity, Volume Eight
    Compiled by Liu Qi of the Yuan Dynasty

    During the Jin Dynasty, the selection of scholars was heavily based on poetry and prose, so scholars often did not have time to read and write other types of literature. I have heard that older scholars would scold their younger disciples for reading the poetry of Su Shi and Huang Tingjian, so scholars focused only on regulated verse. When asked about other types of writing, they would be completely unaware. Occasionally, some scholars began to read and write after passing the imperial examinations, and these were the distinguished scholars. After the Southern Migration, scholars began to focus more on the study of ancient texts and traditions, and they would compose essays and poetry to outdo one another. However, those who had previously specialized in the imperial examinations viewed them as enemies and were deeply divided into two camps, each criticizing the other. Those who wrote poetry and prose would call the scholars of the imperial examinations "those who study for the examinations," while those who studied for the examinations would call the scholars of literature "those who serve as disciples" and mock them for not being skilled in the imperial examinations. They did not realize that the imperial examinations were established by decree to select all-around talented individuals. The poetry essay was used to select candidates with the ability to write imperial edicts and official documents, the poetry essay was used to select candidates with the ability to write poetry in the style of the Book of Songs and Chu Ci, and the policy essay was used to select candidates who could investigate and propose solutions to current political issues.

    129

    经济之业论以攷识鉴之方四者俱工其人才为何如也而学者不知狃于习俗止力为律赋至于诗策论俱不留心其基于为有司者止考赋而不究诗策论也吾尝记故老云泰和间有司攷诗赋已定去取及读策论则止用笔防庙讳御名且数字数与涂注之多寡有司如此欲举子辈専精难矣南渡后赵杨诸公为有司方于策论中取人故士风稍变颇加意策论又于诗赋中亦辨别读书人才以是文风稍振然亦谤议纷纭然每贡举非数公为有司则又如旧矣
    　　金朝以律赋著名者曰孟宗献友之赵枢子克其主文有藻鉴多得人者曰张景仁御史郑子时侍读故一时为之语曰主司非张郑秀才非赵孟律赋至今为学者法然其源出于吾髙祖南山翁故老云孟晚进初不识翁因少年下第发愤辟一室取翁赋剪其八韵之帖壁间坐卧讽咏深思已而尽得其法下笔造微妙再试魁于乡于府于省于御前天下号孟四元【案洪

    Explanation

    129

    Economic professions are discussed to examine methods of identification and evaluation. If all four aspects are skilled, what kind of talent is it? Scholars do not realize they are limited by custom, focusing only on composing regulated verse. As for poetry, policy essays, and arguments, they are not given attention; the fault lies with the examiners who only test composition and do not delve into poetry, policy essays, and arguments. I once heard from old scholars that during the Tahe period, examiners decided on the selection of poetry and composition, and when reading policy essays and arguments, they only used their pens to avoid temple taboos and imperial names, and the number of characters and annotations varied. With examiners like this, it is difficult for candidates to specialize. After the Southern Crossing, officials like Zhao and Yang selected candidates based on policy essays and arguments, so the scholarly风气changed somewhat, and more attention was paid to policy essays and arguments. They also distinguished between reading talents in poetry and composition, thus the literary风气was somewhat revitalized, but there were also many criticisms. However, every time the candidates were selected, if these few officials were not in charge, it would be like the old days again.

    In the Jin Dynasty, those famous for regulated verse were Meng Zongxian, Zhao Shu, and Zhao Ku. Those who had good discernment and often selected talented individuals were Zhang Jingren, the censor, and Zheng Zishi, the tutor. At that time, people said, "If the examiners are not Zhang or Zheng, the scholars are not Zhao or Meng." Regulated verse remains a model for scholars today, but its origins trace back to my great-grandfather, Mr. Nan Shan. Old scholars said that Meng was a latecomer who initially did not know Mr. Nan Shan. Because he failed the exams in his youth, he became determined and set up a room, taking Mr. Nan Shan's regulated verse, cutting it into eight lines, and pasting them on the wall. He recited and pondered them day and night, deeply reflecting until he fully understood the method. When he wrote, he achieved subtlety and passed the exams first in his hometown, the prefecture, the provincial examination, and the imperial examination, earning the title "Meng the Four Yuan." [Note: Yuan refers to the top rank in the imperial examinations.]

    130

    皓松漠纪闻金制乡试以本县令为试官首曰乡元府试分三路首曰府元会试集诸路举人于燕京首曰敕头亦曰状元此盖举天徳以前科举之制故有三元与此志所云四元异及大徳改制始増殿试孟宗献中大定三年进士故有四元之目】迄今学者以吾祖孟师也孟虽仕不甚贵作诗词有可称自号虚静居心颇恬淡留意养生术尝着金丹赋行于世其诗词亦有集
    　　予髙祖南山翁金国初辟进士举词赋状元也故为一代词学宗雅好成就后进见其文辄能断其后中第否当时名士大夫多出门下学者至今皆师尊之四子长西岩次龙泉同年擢第二女长姑及笄将适人一时贵显者争求之翁皆不许张御史景仁时在布衣以所业诣翁翁嘉之俄翁预为有司取士张赋甚佳为邻坐者剽之尽坐

    Explanation

    130

    The Hao Song Mo Ji Wen (皓松漠纪闻) records that during the Jin Dynasty, the local-level imperial examination (乡试) was overseen by the county magistrate, who served as the examiner. The top scorer in this exam was called the "乡元." The prefectural exam was divided into three regions, with the top scorer in each region known as the "府元." The metropolitan exam gathered candidates from all regions in Yanjing (Beijing), and the top scorer in this exam was called the "敕头" or "状元." This system was based on the imperial examination practices before the Tiande period (1150–1153), which is why it had three levels of top scorers, in contrast to the four levels mentioned in other records. The Daode reform (1295–1307) of the Yuan Dynasty introduced the palace exam (殿试), creating the four-tiered system of "四元" (local, prefectural, metropolitan, and palace exam top scorers). Meng Zongxian passed the imperial examination in the third year of the Dading period (1163) as a Jinshi, and his success established the four-tiered system. To this day, scholars regard my ancestor Meng Zongxian as a teacher. Although he did not attain a high official rank, his poetry and verse are worthy of praise. He called himself "Xu Jing Ju" (虚静居) and had a tranquil and unworldly disposition. He was interested in methods of longevity and once wrote the Jin Dan Fu (金丹赋), which circulated in the world. His poetry and verse also have collections.

    My great-grandfather, the "South Mountain Old Man" (南山翁), was selected as the top scorer in the Jinshi examination for prose and poetry at the beginning of the Jin Dynasty. He was a master of literary composition in his generation and had a refined taste for nurturing young scholars. He could predict whether someone would pass the exam by reading their work, and many prominent scholars of the time studied under him. Even today, scholars revere him. He had four sons: the eldest was named Xiyan, the second was named Longquan, and they were both selected as the second-place winners in the same year. His eldest daughter, upon reaching adulthood, was sought after by many prominent and influential people, but the old man refused them all. Zhang Jingren, a censor during the Jin Dynasty, was once a commoner who brought his work to the old man. The old man praised him, and soon the old man was involved in the selection of candidates by the officials. Zhang's work was excellent, but it was plagiarized by someone sitting next to him, and they were all punished.

    131

    同而黜已而翁知其然遽以长姑嫁焉家人辈皆愠翁不恤也后三年翁复为有司御试张擢别试魁骤歴清华以文章擅当世位至翰林学士河南尹御史大夫尝使宋有风节赫然为名臣世皆以翁有知人之鉴也后翁墓表张所作且载其事云次姑适襄隂王元节亦名进士能诗博学尝为宻州节度判官迄今士大夫嫁女多谈公之事也
    　　金朝士大夫以政事最著名者曰王翛然尝同知咸平府摄府事时辽东路多世袭明安穆昆居焉其人皆女直功臣子鷔亢奢纵不法公思有以治之防郡民一世袭明安者钱贫不能偿明安者大怒率家僮辈强入其家牵其牛以去民因讼于官公得其情令一吏呼明安者其明安者盛陈骑从以来公朝服召至厅事前诘其事趋左右械击之乃以强盗论杖杀于市一路悚然后知大兴府素

    Explanation

    131

    The old man initially disagreed but, realizing something, quickly married his eldest daughter to him. The family all resented this, but the old man did not care. Three years later, the old man was again appointed by the imperial examination board and promoted to the top of a special examination. He quickly rose through the ranks of high office, becoming famous for his writings in his time, and reached the positions of Hanlin Scholar, Governor of Henan, and Grand Censor. He once sent someone to the Song Dynasty who had integrity and became a famous minister. The world all considered the old man to have the ability to recognize talent. Later, the tombstone inscription was written by Zhang, and it recorded these events. His second daughter married Wang Yuanjie of Xiangyin, who was also a famous jinshi, skilled in poetry, and well-educated. She once served as the governor of Mizhou. To this day, scholars often discuss the old man's story when marrying their daughters.

    Among the officials of the Jin Dynasty who were most famous for their political affairs was Wang Xiruan. He once served as the deputy governor of Xianping Prefecture and acted as the governor during the absence of the official. At that time, the eastern road of Liao had many hereditary Mingan Muqun residents, who were descendants of Jurchen noble families. These people were proud, extravagant, and lawless. Wang Xiruan thought of a way to govern them. When a hereditary Mingan Muqun resident named Wang was poor and unable to repay a debt, the Mingan Muqun became very angry and led his servants to forcibly enter Wang's home, take his ox, and leave. The people then sued the case in court. Wang Xiruan investigated the matter and ordered an official to summon the Mingan Muqun. The Mingan Muqun arrived with a grand entourage. Wang Xiruan, dressed in court attire, summoned him to the front of the hall, questioned him about the matter, and ordered the guards to arrest and strike him. He was then sentenced as a robber and flogged to death in the market, causing the entire road to be shocked. Later, he served as the governor of Daxing Prefecture, known for his integrity and wisdom.

    132

    察僧徒多游贵戚作过乃下令午后僧不得出寺街中不见一僧有一长老犯禁公械之长老者素为贵戚所重皇姑某国公主使人诣公请焉公曰奉王命即令出立召僧杖一百死自是京辇肃清人莫敢犯世宗深见知故公得行其志也公为人恬淡简静颇留意养生每食必以时过午则不食也临终斋沐而逝于死生了然其为吏之名至今人云过宋包拯其子渐为吏亦有能称为都中警巡使
    　　孙左丞铎振之章宗时名臣为人正直敢言有学问文采一时相望甚切俄诏下同辈皆相执政公再授戸部尚书公意不惬因于戸部防事壁间书唐人诗云【案中州集传孙铎再为戸部尚书于贺席中戏举青州老柏院布衣张在诗云云与此志所云书于戸部防壁有异】南邻北舎牡丹开年少寻芳去未囘惟有君家老柏树春风来似不曽来有人奏之坐贬鄜州防御使【案中州集传铎既为御史所劾降授河南同知府事故送之者有云想到洛阳春正好南邻北里牡丹开与此志所云贬鄜州防御有异】再召入朝未几执政南渡为太子太师后致仕以寿终

    Explanation

    132

    The official noticed that monks frequently visited noble families and caused trouble, so he issued an order prohibiting monks from leaving the temple after noon, resulting in no monks being seen in the streets. One senior monk violated this rule, and the official had him bound. This senior monk was highly regarded by the nobility, including a princess of a certain country, who had someone go to the official to plead on his behalf. The official replied, "Following the king's command, I will have the monk flogged to death." After this, the capital was cleared of trouble, and no one dared to violate the rules. Emperor Shizong deeply understood the official, allowing him to carry out his intentions. The official was calm and simple, paying attention to health, and ate only before noon. He passed away after a ritual bath, having a clear understanding of life and death, and his reputation as an official was so great that people compared him to Song Dynasty's Bao Zheng, and his son also became a capable official, serving as the Chief Inspector of the Capital.

    Left Chancellor Sun Diao, a prominent minister during Emperor Zhangzong's reign, was upright, courageous, and knowledgeable, with literary talent. His contemporaries greatly respected him, but after a short time, an imperial decree was issued, and his peers all took up positions in the government. He was then reappointed as Minister of Revenue, which he was not satisfied with, so he wrote a poem by a Tang Dynasty poet on the wall of the Revenue Department: "South of the neighbor's house, peonies bloom; young people go out to enjoy the flowers, but do not return. Only the old pine tree in your courtyard remains unchanged by the spring wind." Someone reported this poem, and as a result, he was demoted to the position of Defense Commissioner of Fuzhou. He was recalled to court again, but soon after, he moved south to serve as Grand Tutor to the Crown Prince, retired, and died of old age.

    133

    贞祐南征获一统制官李申之者帅府经歴官刘达卿辈召而饭之且诱以降将宥焉申之献诗曰一饭感恩无地报此心许国已天知胸中千古蟠钟阜一死鸿毛断不移竟就死又云拟把孤忠报主知主知未报己身疲明朝定作长淮鬼马革应烦为裹尸又云区区已上和亲防安得元戎一防头【案中州集王或诗注贞祐末行台都尉南征获武经进士李申之于盱胎左右司郎中刘光谦达卿润文官李献能钦叔爱其才辩欲活之以避嫌不敢也乃托以问事机令军中羁管之申之作诗赠主囚者曰一饭感君无地报寸心许国只天知明朝定

    Explanation

    133

    During the Zhenyou era's southern campaign, a commander named Li Shen was captured. Officials in the military command, including Liu Dacheng, invited him to eat and tried to persuade him to surrender with a promise of leniency. Li Shen responded with the following poem:

    "Grateful for a single meal, unable to repay, this heart is devoted to the country, known even by heaven. Thousands of years of loyalty and righteousness are deeply rooted in my heart. To die as light as a feather, but my resolve will not change. Intending to offer loyal service to the emperor, a small effort to prevent peace negotiations. How can one obtain a command from the supreme commander?"

    Li Shen then said, "I intend to offer loyal service to the emperor, but I have not yet fulfilled my duty to the emperor. Tomorrow, I will become a ghost of the Yangtze River, and my body will be wrapped in horsehide."

    He also said, "A small effort to prevent peace negotiations, how can one obtain a command from the supreme commander?"

    134

    作长淮鬼马革仍烦为裹尸又云胸中万古横钟阜一死鸿毛断不移又献书都尉云金国嵗嵗南侵计所得不能一二州而军力折耗殆尽今歳此举亦曽虑人有议其后何以御之乎为公计者不若因南军大举敛兵而退虽屡出无功得全师而返犹可自救不然师老食殚困顿于坚城之下谗间一行则公受祸不久矣某军败而死固其所也乞于盱眙城下责以不降之罪以死见处使人人知之则都尉亦于名教为有功书上之明日申之谋遁归不果乃杀之钦叔説其临刑囘面南向欣然就戮甚嗟惜之云云与此志所纪微异而较详并附载之】

    Explanation

    134

    Becoming a ghost in the Changhuai region, the horsehide is still used to wrap the body. It is also said that one's spirit remains vast and enduring like the ancient hills, and a single death, like a feather, cannot be moved. Another letter was presented to the都尉, stating that the Jin Dynasty invades the south year after year, but the gains are limited to only a few states, and military strength has been severely depleted. This year's action was also considered with concern for what might be said afterward, and how to defend against such actions. For the sake of the general, it would be better to withdraw troops due to the large-scale southern army, even if there is no success, returning with the entire army intact could still be beneficial. Otherwise, the army would become exhausted and depleted while besieging a strong city, and if slander and intrigue occur, the person will soon suffer misfortune. The defeat and death of the army were inevitable. A request was made to hold someone accountable for refusing to surrender and face execution, so that everyone would know, and the都尉 would also be seen as having contributed to the moral order. After submitting the document, plans to escape were unsuccessful, leading to execution.钦叔 said that the person faced execution with a smile, turning to face south, and expressed great regret. This account differs slightly from what is recorded but is more detailed and is included as an appendix.

    135

    先翰林尝谈国初宇文太学叔通主文盟时呉深州彦髙视宇文为后进宇文止呼为小呉因防饮酒间有一妇人宋宗室子流落诸公感叹皆作乐章一阕宇文作念奴娇有宗室家姬陈王幼女曽嫁钦慈族干戈浩荡事随天地翻覆之语次及彦髙作人月圆词云南朝千古伤心事犹唱后庭花旧时王谢堂前燕子飞向谁家偶然相见仙
    肌胜雪云髩堆鸦江州司马青衫湿泪同是天涯宇文览之大惊自是人乞词辄曰当诣彦髙也彦髙词集篇数虽不多皆精微尽善虽多用前人诗句其剪截缀防若天成真奇作也先人尝云诗不宜用前人语若夫乐章则剪截古人语亦无害但要能使用尔如彦髙人月圆半是古人句其思致含蓄甚逺不露圭角不犹胜于宇文自作者哉

    Explanation

    135

    In the early days, the Grand Secretary Yu Wen once discussed with others that Yu Wen, a prominent scholar, led the literary community. Wu Shenzhou of Shen Prefecture, who viewed Yu Wen as a younger scholar, was affectionately called "Little Wu" by him. During a drinking gathering, there was a woman, a noblewoman from the Song royal family, who had fallen into misfortune. The scholars were deeply moved and all composed a musical piece in her honor. Yu Wen wrote a poem in the form of "Nian Nujiao" (念奴娇), which included lines about a noblewoman from the Chen royal family, a young daughter who had been married to a respected and benevolent clan, and the turbulent times that caused her fate to be as unpredictable as the changes in the heavens and earth. Later, Wu Shenzhou wrote a poem in the form of "Ren Yue Yuan" (人月圆), which reflected on the tragic history of the Southern Dynasties, mentioning the song "Hou Ting Hua" (Flowers of the Rear Palace) and the swallows of the Wang and Xie families, questioning where they would fly now. They met by chance, and the encounter was profound, with the woman's skin as pure and white as snow and her hair as black and piled up like crows. The tears of the Jiangzhou Magistrate, wetting his green robe, were the same for those who were far from home. Yu Wen was greatly surprised upon reading Wu Shenzhou's poem and decided that whenever someone asked for a poem, he would say, "I should consult Wu Shenzhou." Although Wu Shenzhou's collection of poems was not extensive, each piece was meticulously crafted and of high quality. He often used phrases from previous works, but his skillful use of these phrases made them seem as if they were naturally his own, truly remarkable creations. My ancestors once said that poetry should not use phrases from previous works, but in the case of musical compositions, using and adapting phrases from the ancients is harmless, as long as one can use them effectively. For example, in Wu Shenzhou's "Ren Yue Yuan," half of the poem consists of phrases from previous works, yet the thoughts are deep and subtle, reaching far, and the style is smooth and polished, not revealing any sharp edges. This is certainly superior to Yu Wen's original compositions.

    136

    党承防怀英辛尚书弃疾俱山东人少同舎属金国初遭乱俱在兵间辛一旦率数千骑南渡显于宋党在北方擢第入翰林有名为一时文字宗主二公虽所趣不同皆有功业宠荣视前朝陶谷韩熙载亦相况也后辛退闲有词鹧鸪天云壮歳旌旗拥万夫锦襜突骑渡江初燕兵夜娖银胡防汉箭朝飞金仆姑追往事叹今吾春秋不染白髭却将万字平戎策换得东家种树书盖纪其少时事也
    　　髙丞相岩夫在相位因元光二年元日庆七十防乡里交旧且求作诗文时先子以新罢御史避嫌不赴余方弱冠为作诗以公颇谤且劝其退休也公得诗大喜趣召余迎谓余曰解道青云自致不须阶耶又抚余背曰汝曹字如何下来盖余诗云青云自致不须阶十稔从容位上台荷一堂森柱石调和众口费盐梅勤劳密迩三朝重寿考康寜七秩开家道益昌孙有息防衣扶杖好归来雷希顔为作序亦有乗天眷未衰可以引去之语后余将归淮复献书劝其举一人自代可得致政归然公竟薨相位不能从也

    Explanation

    136

    Party Chengfang and Xin Qiji, both from Shandong, grew up together during the early years of the Jin Dynasty when chaos erupted. Both were in the military at the time. Xin Qiji once led thousands of cavalry south across the river and became famous in the Song Dynasty. Party Chengfang, who remained in the north, passed the imperial examinations and entered the Hanlin Academy, becoming famous and a leading figure in literature of his time. Despite their different pursuits, both achieved fame and honor, comparable to the achievements of Tao Gu and Han Xizai from the previous dynasty. Later, after Xin Qiji retired, he wrote a poem called "Zhegu Tian" (Partridge Sky), which reads:

    In his youth, he led a thousand men with banners flying, crossing the river with armored cavalry. Jin soldiers prepared their bows at night, while Han arrows flew in the morning.
